Okay, so I have this question about isomers, specifically constitutional isomers and stereoisomers. It mentions two types: tautomerism and optical isomerism. The question is asking about two things:

A) Which compound between benzoquinone and cyclohexane-1,3,5-trione does not show tautomerism.

B) Among methyl 2-hydroxypropanoate and dimethyl fumarate, which one will show optical isomerism.

And the given answers are A = cyclohexane-1,3,5-trione and B = methyl 2-hydroxypropanoate. But I need to figure out why that's the case.

First, let's break down what each term means.

**Tautomerism**: This occurs when a molecule can shift the position of a hydrogen (or hydrogens) and a pi bond (or bonds) to form a different structure, but with the same molecular formula. For example, in keto-enol tautomerism, a carbonyl group (C=O) can become an enol form (C=C-OH). So, the molecule can exist in two or more structurally different forms.

**Optical Isomerism**: This occurs when a molecule has multiple stereocenters and can exist in mirror-image forms, making it chiral. These are also called stereoisomers, and they have different physical properties, like melting point and boiling point.

** Constitutional Isomers**: These have the same molecular formula but different connectivity of atoms. For example, benzene and its structural isomers like cyclohexene or other ring structures.

** Stereoisomers**: These have the same connectivity of atoms but different spatial arrangements. The classic example is cis and trans butane.

Alright, so I have this chemistry question about isomers, specifically tautomerism and optical isomerism. Let me try to break it down step by step.

The question is asking about two compounds: benzoquinone and cyclohexane-1,3,5-trione. It wants to know which one doesn't show tautomerism, and then between methyl 2-hydroxypropanoate and dimethyl fumarate, which one shows optical isomerism.

First, let's recall what tautomerism is. Tautomerism involves the shifting of a hydrogen and a pair of electrons to form a different structural formula, like how ketones can tautomerize to enols. So, if a compound can exist in different forms by such a shift, it shows tautomerism.

For the first part, identifying which compound doesn't show tautomerism. The compounds given are benzoquinone and cyclohexane-1,3,5-trione.

Benzoquinone is a benzene ring with two ketone groups. I remember that quinones can have enol forms, so benzoquinone can tautomerize to form something like a dienol or a conjugated enol. So, benzoquinone does show tautomerism.

Cyclohexane-1,3,5-trione, on the other hand, has three ketone groups at the 1, 3, and 5 positions of a cyclohexane ring. Let me visualize this. It's like a six-membered ring with three carbonyl groups. Now, can this compound tautomerize? Well, tautomerism usually requires the presence of enolizable hydrogens. In this case, each carbonyl group is adjacent to two CH2 groups. So, each carbonyl has two hydrogens attached to the alpha carbon. If a hydrogen is moved along with a pair of electrons to the adjacent carbonyl, would that form a more stable structure?

Wait, let me think. In cyclohexane-1,3,5-trione, all three carbonyls are at positions 1, 3, and 5, which are every other carbon in the ring. The hydrogen on position 2 can move to the carbonyl carbon at position 1, but wait, isn't that the same as the original structure? Hmm, maybe not. Because in the enol form, the double bond would be between the carbonyl carbon (position 1) and the alpha carbon (position 2). But in the original compound, each carbonyl is already part of a conjugated system.

Positions 1, 3, 5 are carbonyls. Each carbonyl is at a position where the adjacent carbons are CH2 groups. So, for position 1, carbons 2 and 6 are CH2. So, wait, can a carbon have two double bonds? In this case, it's a six-membered ring. If carbon 1 is double-bonded to both carbon 2 and carbon 6? But that would require all three carbons to be double-bonded, which isn't possible in a six-membered ring because that would create a cumulene, which is a linear chain with alternating double bonds, but a ring can't have that.

So, actually, cyclohexane-1,3,5-trione doesn't have any enolizable hydrogens because each carbonyl is isolated by two CH2 groups. So, there's no possibility of a hydrogen shifting to form an enol or any tautomer. So, cyclohexane-1,3,5-trione doesn't exhibit tautomerism. First, understanding the concepts involved. Tautomerism refers to the phenomenon where two structurally different compounds can interconvert by rearranging bonds (like keto-enol tautomerism). Optical isomerism, on the other hand, occurs when a compound has multiple stereogenic centers, causing the molecule to have non-superimposable mirror images, leading to optical isomerism.

Okay, so I have this question about isomers, specifically about tautomerism and optical isomerism. Let me try to understand what's being asked here.

First, the question mentions two types of isomers: constitutional isomers and stereoisomers. Constitutional isomers have the same molecular formula but different structures, while stereoisomers are compounds that are non-superimposable mirror images of each other, meaning they can have optical isomerism.

The question is divided into two parts, A and B.
